<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

<p><b>Mariah Carey</b> (born March 27, 1969 or 1970)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> is an American singer, songwriter, record producer, and occasional actress. Born and raised on <a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">Long Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York" title="New York">New York</a>, Carey came to prominence after releasing her self-titled debut <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Studio_album" title="Studio album">studio album</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Mariah_Carey_(album)" title="Mariah Carey (album)">Mariah Carey</a></i> in 1990; it went multiplatinum and spawned four consecutive number one singles on the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a> chart. Under the guidance of <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Records" title="Columbia Records">Columbia Records</a> executive and later husband <a href="/wiki/Tommy_Mottola" title="Tommy Mottola">Tommy Mottola</a>, she continued booking success with followup albums <i><a href="/wiki/Emotions_(Mariah_Carey_album)" title="Emotions (Mariah Carey album)">Emotions</a></i> (1991), <i><a href="/wiki/Music_Box_(Mariah_Carey_album)" title="Music Box (Mariah Carey album)">Music Box</a></i> (1993), and <i><a href="/wiki/Merry_Christmas_(Mariah_Carey_album)" title="Merry Christmas (Mariah Carey album)">Merry Christmas</a></i> (1994), Carey was established as Columbia's highest-selling act. <i><a href="/wiki/Daydream_(Mariah_Carey_album)" title="Daydream (Mariah Carey album)">Daydream</a></i> (1995) made music history when its second single "<a href="/wiki/One_Sweet_Day" title="One Sweet Day">One Sweet Day</a>", a duet with <a href="/wiki/Boyz_II_Men" title="Boyz II Men">Boyz II Men</a>, spent a record sixteen weeks on top of the <i>Billboard</i> Hot 100, and remains the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Billboard_Hot_100_chart_achievements_and_milestones" title="List of Billboard Hot 100 chart achievements and milestones">longest-running number-one song in U.S. chart history</a>. During the recording of the album, Carey began to deviate from her <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_R%26B" title="Contemporary R&amp;B">R&amp;B</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">pop</a> beginnings and slowly traversed into hip hop. This musical change became evident with the release of <i><a href="/wiki/Butterfly_(Mariah_Carey_album)" title="Butterfly (Mariah Carey album)">Butterfly</a></i> (1997), at which time Carey had separated from Mottola.</p>
<p>Carey left Columbia in 2000, and signed a $100 million recording contract with <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Records" title="Virgin Records">Virgin Records America</a>. Before the release of her film <i><a href="/wiki/Glitter_(film)" title="Glitter (film)">Glitter</a></i> (2001), she suffered a physical and emotional breakdown and was hospitalized for severe exhaustion. Following the film's poor reception, she was bought out of her recording contract for $50 million, which led to a decline in her career. She signed a multimillion dollar contract deal with <a href="/wiki/Island_Records" title="Island Records">Island Records</a> in 2002, and after an unsuccessful period, returned to the top of music charts with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Emancipation_of_Mimi" title="The Emancipation of Mimi">The Emancipation of Mimi</a></i> (2005). Its second single "<a href="/wiki/We_Belong_Together" title="We Belong Together">We Belong Together</a>" became her most successful single of the 2000s, and was later named "Song of the Decade" by <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i>. Carey once again ventured into film with a well-received supporting role in <i><a href="/wiki/Precious_(film)" title="Precious (film)">Precious</a></i> (2009); she was awarded the "Breakthrough Performance Award" at the <a href="/wiki/Palm_Springs_International_Film_Festival" title="Palm Springs International Film Festival">Palm Springs International Film Festival</a>, and received <a href="/wiki/Black_Reel_Awards_of_2010" title="Black Reel Awards of 2010">Black Reel</a> and <a href="/wiki/NAACP_Image_Award" title="NAACP Image Award">NAACP Image Award</a> nominations.</p>
<p>Throughout her career, Carey has sold more than 200 million records worldwide, making her one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_music_artists" title="List of best-selling music artists">best-selling music artists</a> of all time. In 1998, she was honored as the world's best-selling recording artist of the 1990s at the <a href="/wiki/World_Music_Awards" title="World Music Awards">World Music Awards</a>. Carey was also named the best-selling female artist of the millennium in 2000. According to the <a href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America" title="Recording Industry Association of America">Recording Industry Association of America</a>, she is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_music_artists_in_the_United_States" title="List of best-selling music artists in the United States">third-best-selling female artist</a> in the United States, with 63.5 million <a href="/wiki/RIAA_certification" title="RIAA certification">certified</a> albums. With the release of "<a href="/wiki/Touch_My_Body_(Mariah_Carey_song)" title="Touch My Body (Mariah Carey song)">Touch My Body</a>" (2008), Carey gained her 18th number-one single in the United States, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_artists_by_number_of_Billboard_Hot_100_number_one_singles" title="List of artists by number of Billboard Hot 100 number one singles">more than any other solo artist</a>. In 2012, Carey was ranked second on <a href="/wiki/VH1" title="VH1">VH1</a>'s list of the "100 Greatest Women in Music". Aside from her commercial accomplishments, Carey has won five <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award" title="Grammy Award">Grammy Awards</a>, 19 <a href="/wiki/World_Music_Awards" title="World Music Awards">World Music Awards</a>, 11 <a href="/wiki/American_Music_Award" title="American Music Award">American Music Awards</a>, and 31 <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Music_Award" title="Billboard Music Award">Billboard Music Awards</a>. Referred to as the "songbird supreme" by the <i><a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness World Records</a></i>, she is famed for her five-octave <a href="/wiki/Vocal_range" title="Vocal range">vocal range</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belting_(music)" title="Belting (music)">power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melisma" title="Melisma">melismatic</a> style and signature use of the <a href="/wiki/Whistle_register" title="Whistle register">whistle register</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<p>Mariah Carey was born on March 27, 1969 or 1970 on <a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">Long Island</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Huntington,_New_York" title="Huntington, New York">Huntington, New York</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick1_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick1-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> Her father, Alfred Roy, was of <a href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African American</a> and <a href="/wiki/Venezuelan_people" title="Venezuelan people">Venezuelan</a> (including Afro-Venezuelan) descent, while her mother, Patricia (Hickey), is of White <a href="/wiki/Irish_American" title="Irish American">Irish</a> descent. The last name Carey was adopted by her Venezuelan grandfather, Francisco Núñez, after emigrating to New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick2_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick2-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Patricia was an occasional opera singer and vocal coach before she met Alfred in 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick1_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-nick1-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> As he began earning a living as an aeronautical engineer, the couple wed later that year, and moved into a small suburb in New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick2_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-nick2-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> After their elopement, Patricia's family disowned her due to her marrying a black man. Carey later explained that growing up, she felt a notion of neglect from her maternal family, a mark that affected her greatly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick2_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-nick2-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> During the years between the births of Carey's older sister Alison and herself, the Carey family struggled within the community due to their ethnicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick2_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-nick2-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Carey's name was derived from the song "<a href="/wiki/They_Call_the_Wind_Maria" title="They Call the Wind Maria">They Call the Wind Maria</a>", originally from the 1951 Broadway musical <i><a href="/wiki/Paint_Your_Wagon_(musical)" title="Paint Your Wagon (musical)">Paint Your Wagon</a>.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> When Carey was three, her parents divorced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick3_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick3-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:300px; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"It's been difficult for me, moving around so much, having to grow up by myself... my parents divorced. And I always felt kind of different from everybody else in my neighborhoods. I was a different person ethnically. And sometimes, that can be a problem. If you look a certain way, everybody goes 'White girl', and I'd go, 'No, that's not what I am'."</p>
</div>
</div>
<div style="text-align: left;">—Carey, on her childhood<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick6_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick6-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
<p>After their separation, Alison moved in with her father, while the other two children, Mariah and brother Morgan, remained with their mother. Carey would grow apart from her father, and would later stop seeing him altogether.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick3_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-nick3-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick4_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick4-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> By the age of four, Carey recalled that she had begun to sneak the radio under her covers at night, and just sing and try and find peace within the music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick4_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-nick4-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> During elementary school, she excelled in subjects that she enjoyed, such as music, art, and literature, but did not find interest in others. After several years of financial struggles, Patricia earned enough money to move her family into a stable and more affluent sector in New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick5_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick5-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Carey had begun writing poems and adding melodies to them, thus starting as a <a href="/wiki/Singer-songwriter" title="Singer-songwriter">singer-songwriter</a> while attending <a href="/wiki/Harborfields_High_School" title="Harborfields High School">Harborfields High School</a> in Greenlawn, New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick7_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick7-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Carey excelled in her music, and demonstrated usage of the <a href="/wiki/Whistle_register" title="Whistle register">whistle register</a>, though only beginning to master and control it through her training with her mother. Though introducing her daughter to classical opera, Patricia never pressured her to pursue a career in it, as she never seemed interested. Carey recalled that she kept her singer-songwriter works a secret and noted that Patricia had "never been a pushy mom. She never said, 'Give it more of an operatic feel'. I respect opera like crazy, but it didn't influence me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick7_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-nick7-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick8_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick8-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>While in high school, Carey began writing songs with <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Christopher" title="Gavin Christopher">Gavin Christopher</a>. They needed an assistant who could play the <a href="/wiki/Musical_keyboard" title="Musical keyboard">keyboard</a>: "We called someone and he couldn't come, so by accident we stumbled upon <a href="/wiki/Ben_Margulies" title="Ben Margulies">Ben</a> [Margulies]. Ben came to the studio, and he really couldn't play the keyboards very well — he was really more of a drummer — but after that day, we kept in touch, and we sort of clicked as writers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick8_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-nick8-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> Carey and Christopher began writing and composing songs in the basement of his father's store during Carey's <a href="/wiki/Senior_(education)" title="Senior (education)">senior year</a>. After composing their first song together, "Here We Go Round Again", which Carey described as having a <a href="/wiki/Motown" title="Motown">Motown</a> vibe, they continued writing material for a full-length demo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick9_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick9-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> She began living in a one-bedroom apartment in Manhattan, which she shared with four other female students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick10_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick10-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick11_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick11-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> Carey worked as a waitress for various restaurants, usually getting fired after two weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick12_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick12-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> While requiring work to pay for her rent, Carey still had musical ambitions, as she continued working late into the night with Margulies in hopes of completing a demo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick12_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-nick12-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> After completing her four song demo tape, Carey attempted to pass it to music labels, but failed each time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick13_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick13-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> Shortly thereafter, she was introduced to rising <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">pop</a> singer <a href="/wiki/Brenda_K._Starr" title="Brenda K. Starr">Brenda K. Starr</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick13_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-nick13-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick14_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick14-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1988.E2.80.9392:_Mariah_Carey_and_Emotions">1988–92: <i>Mariah Carey</i> and <i>Emotions</i></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mariah_Carey_1990.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="554" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Mariah_Carey_1990.jpg/170px-Mariah_Carey_1990.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Mariah_Carey_1990.jpg/255px-Mariah_Carey_1990.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Mariah_Carey_1990.jpg/340px-Mariah_Carey_1990.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mariah_Carey_1990.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Carey exiting the Shepherd's Bush Theatre after promoting her single "Vision of Love" on <i>The Wogan Show</i>, in 1990</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As Starr's friendship with Carey grew, so did her interest in helping Carey succeed in the industry. In December 1988, Carey accompanied Starr to a record executives' gala, where she handed her demo tape to <a href="/wiki/Tommy_Mottola" title="Tommy Mottola">Tommy Mottola</a>, head of <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Records" title="Columbia Records">Columbia Records</a>, who listened to it on his way back home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick17_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick17-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick16_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick16-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> After the first two songs, he was so enamored of Carey's voice that he returned to the event, only to find that she had left.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick16_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-nick16-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> In what has been widely described by critics as a modern day <a href="/wiki/Cinderella" title="Cinderella">Cinderella</a> tale,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nycc_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-nycc-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> after searching for Carey for two weeks, he immediately signed her and began mapping out her commercial debut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick17_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-nick17-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> While she maintained that she wanted to continue working with Margulies, Mottola enlisted top producers of the time, including <a href="/wiki/Ric_Wake" title="Ric Wake">Ric Wake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Narada_Michael_Walden" title="Narada Michael Walden">Narada Michael Walden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhett_Lawrence" title="Rhett Lawrence">Rhett Lawrence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick17_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-nick17-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> Mottola and the staff at Columbia had planned to market Carey as their main female pop artist, competing with <a href="/wiki/Whitney_Houston" title="Whitney Houston">Whitney Houston</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madonna_(entertainer)" title="Madonna (entertainer)">Madonna</a> (signed to <a href="/wiki/Arista_Records" title="Arista Records">Arista</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sire_Records" title="Sire Records">Sire Records</a> respectively).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick18_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick18-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> After the completion of her debut album, <i><a href="/wiki/Mariah_Carey_(album)" title="Mariah Carey (album)">Mariah Carey</a></i>, Columbia spent more than $1 million promoting it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick19_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick19-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite a weak start, the album eventually reached the top of the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a>, after Carey's exposure at the <a href="/wiki/33rd_Annual_Grammy_Awards" title="33rd Annual Grammy Awards">33rd Annual Grammy Awards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick20_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick20-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Mariah Carey</i> stayed atop the charts for eleven consecutive weeks,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (February 2015)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup> and she won the <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_New_Artist" title="Grammy Award for Best New Artist">Best New Artist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Female_Pop_Vocal_Performance" title="Grammy Award for Best Female Pop Vocal Performance">Best Female Pop Vocal Performance</a> awards for her single "<a href="/wiki/Vision_of_Love" title="Vision of Love">Vision of Love</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> The album yielded three more number one singles on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a>, following the four-week number-one run of "Vision of Love". Carey became the first artist since <a href="/wiki/The_Jackson_5" title="The Jackson 5">The Jackson 5</a> to have their first four singles reach number one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Mariah Carey</i> finished as the best-selling album in the United States in 1991,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> while totaling sales of over 15 million copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bet_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-bet-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Carey began recording her second studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/Emotions_(Mariah_Carey_album)" title="Emotions (Mariah Carey album)">Emotions</a></i>, in 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick21_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick21-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick22_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick22-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> She described it as a homage to <a href="/wiki/Motown" title="Motown">Motown</a> <a href="/wiki/Soul_music" title="Soul music">soul music</a>, as she felt the need to pay tribute to the type of music that had influenced her as a child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick22_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-nick22-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> For the project, Carey worked with <a href="/wiki/Walter_Afanasieff" title="Walter Afanasieff">Walter Afanasieff</a>, who only had a small role on her debut, as well as <a href="/wiki/Robert_Clivill%C3%A9s" title="Robert Clivillés">Robert Clivillés</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Cole_(record_producer)" title="David Cole (record producer)">David Cole</a>, from the <a href="/wiki/Dance_music" title="Dance music">dance</a> group <a href="/wiki/C%2BC_Music_Factory" title="C+C Music Factory">C+C Music Factory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick23_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick23-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> However, Carey's relationship with Margulies deteriorated over a contract Carey had signed before signing with <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Records" title="Columbia Records">Columbia</a>, agreeing to split not only the songwriting royalties from the songs, but half of her earnings as well. However, when the time came to write music for <i>Emotions,</i> Sony officials made it clear he would only be paid the fair amount given to co-writers on an album. Margulies later filed a lawsuit against Sony which ultimately led to their parting of ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick22_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-nick22-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Emotions</i> was released on September 17, 1991, and was accepted by critics as a more mature album than its predecessor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick24_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick24-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> While praised for Carey's improved songwriting, production, and new sound, the album was criticized for its material, thought weaker than that of her debut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick25_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick25-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> Though the album managed sales of over eight million copies globally, <i>Emotions</i> failed to reach the commercial and critical heights of its predecessor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sales_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-sales-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As after the release of her debut, critics again questioned whether Carey would embark on a world tour to promote her material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro1_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro1-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> Although Carey explained that <a href="/wiki/Stage_fright" title="Stage fright">stage fright</a> and the style of her songs made a tour very daunting, speculation grew that Carey was a "studio worm", and that she was incapable of producing the perfect pitch and 5-<a href="/wiki/Octave" title="Octave">octave</a> <a href="/wiki/Vocal_range" title="Vocal range">vocal range</a> for which she was known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nycc_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-nycc-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro2_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro2-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> In hopes of putting to rest any claims of her being a manufactured artist, Carey and <a href="/wiki/Walter_Afanasieff" title="Walter Afanasieff">Walter Afanasieff</a> decided to book an appearance on <a href="/wiki/MTV_Unplugged" title="MTV Unplugged">MTV Unplugged</a>, a television program aired by <a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">MTV</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick26_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-nick26-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> The show presented name artists "unplugged" or stripped of studio equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick26_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-nick26-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> While Carey felt<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="You can help — (September 2014)">vague</span></a></i>]</sup>strongly of her more soulful and powerful songs, it was decided that her most popular content would be included. Days before the show's taping, Carey and Afanasieff thought of adding a cover version of an older song, in order to provide something different and unexpected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro3_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro3-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> They chose "<a href="/wiki/I%27ll_Be_There_(The_Jackson_5_song)" title="I'll Be There (The Jackson 5 song)">I'll Be There</a>", a song made popular by <a href="/wiki/The_Jackson_5" title="The Jackson 5">The Jackson 5</a> in 1970. On March 16, 1992, Carey recorded a seven-piece set-list at <a href="/wiki/Kaufman_Astoria_Studios" title="Kaufman Astoria Studios">Kaufman Astoria Studios</a> in Queens, New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson27_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-nickson27-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Revue" title="Revue">revue</a> was met with critical acclaim, leading to it being aired more than three times as often as an average episode would.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson28_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-nickson28-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> The success tempted <a href="/wiki/Sony_Music_Entertainment" title="Sony Music Entertainment">Sony</a> officials to market it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson29_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-nickson29-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> Sony decided to release it as an <a href="/wiki/Extended_play" title="Extended play">EP</a>, priced low because it was short. The EP proved to be a success, contrary to critics and speculations that Carey was just a studio artist,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson30_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-nickson30-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> and was given a triple-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RIAA_Certification" title="RIAA Certification">Platinum</a> certification by the <a href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America" title="Recording Industry Association of America">Recording Industry Association of America</a> (RIAA),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riaa_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-riaa-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> and managed Gold and Platinum certifications in several European markets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson29_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-nickson29-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1993.E2.80.9396:_Music_Box.2C_international_breakthrough.2C_and_Daydream">1993–96: <i>Music Box</i>, international breakthrough, and <i>Daydream</i></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:300px; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"The writing partnership that her and I had and I can't speak for her other songwriting partners, but if you could see us in the room I would hit a chord and play a little melody on the piano and she would say, 'Oh, that's nice,' and she would sing that melody and then she adds a little bit to it. I would then play it back and then she would say, 'Yea, that's good' so it instantly becomes this partnership where eventually she'll have a melody and then the melody would prompt her to start thinking about this feeling she wants to put into words. This would eventually become the theme of the song."</p>
</div>
</div>
<div style="text-align: left;">—Afanasieff, on his songwriting partnership with Carey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-soul_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-soul-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
<p>During early 1993, Carey began working on her third studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/Music_Box_(Mariah_Carey_album)" title="Music Box (Mariah Carey album)">Music Box</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro4_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro4-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> After <i>Emotions</i> failed to achieve the commercial heights of her debut album, Carey and Columbia came to the agreement that the next album would contain a more pop influenced sound, in order to appeal to a wider audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro4_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro4-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> During Carey's writing sessions, she began working mostly with Afanasieff, with whom she co-wrote and produced most of <i>Music Box</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-soul_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-soul-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> On August 31, <i>Music Box</i> was released around the world, debuting at number-one on the <i>Billboard</i> 200.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro7_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro7-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> The album was met with mixed reception from music critics; while many praised the album's pop influence and strong content, others felt that Carey made less usage of her acclaimed vocal range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro8_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro8-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> Ron Wynn from <a href="/wiki/AllMusic" title="AllMusic">AllMusic</a> described Carey's different form of singing on the album: "It was wise for Carey to display other elements of her approach, but sometimes excessive spirit is preferable to an absence of passion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> The album's second single, "<a href="/wiki/Hero_(Mariah_Carey_song)" title="Hero (Mariah Carey song)">Hero</a>", would eventually come to be one of Carey's most popular and inspirational songs of her career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson31_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-nickson31-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> The song became Carey's eighth chart topper in the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson31_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-nickson31-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> and began expanding Carey's popularity throughout Europe. With the release of the album's third single, Carey achieved several career milestones. Her cover of <a href="/wiki/Badfinger" title="Badfinger">Badfinger</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Without_You_(Badfinger_song)" title="Without You (Badfinger song)">Without You</a>" became her first number one single in Germany, Sweden,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ger_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-ger-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> and the United Kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-without_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-without-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>Music Box</i> spent prolonged periods at number one on the album charts of several countries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson32_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-nickson32-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> and eventually became one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_albums" title="List of best-selling albums">best-selling albums</a> of all time, with worldwide sales of over 32 million copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-soul_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-soul-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> After declining to tour for her past two albums, Carey agreed to embark on a short stateside string of concerts, titled the <a href="/wiki/Music_Box_Tour" title="Music Box Tour">Music Box Tour</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro9_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro9-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Spanning only six dates across North America,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro9_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro9-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> the short but successful tour was a large step for Carey, who dreaded the hassle of touring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro10_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro10-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> Following <i>Music Box</i>, Carey took a relatively large period of time away from the public eye, and began working on an unknown project throughout 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro11_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro11-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> In October 1994, <i>Billboard</i> announced that Carey would release a holiday album later that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro11_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro11-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> That 1994, Carey recorded a duet with <a href="/wiki/Luther_Vandross" title="Luther Vandross">Luther Vandross</a>; a cover of <a href="/wiki/Lionel_Richie" title="Lionel Richie">Lionel Richie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diana_Ross" title="Diana Ross">Diana Ross</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Endless_Love_(song)" title="Endless Love (song)">Endless Love</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson33_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-nickson33-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> Carey's album <i><a href="/wiki/Merry_Christmas_(Mariah_Carey_album)" title="Merry Christmas (Mariah Carey album)">Merry Christmas</a></i> was released on November 1, 1994, on the same day that the album's first single, "<a href="/wiki/All_I_Want_for_Christmas_Is_You" title="All I Want for Christmas Is You">All I Want for Christmas Is You</a>", was released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson34_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-nickson34-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> The album eventually became the <a href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_Christmas/holiday_albums_in_the_United_States" title="List of best-selling Christmas/holiday albums in the United States">best-selling Christmas album</a> of all time, with global sales reaching over 15 million copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newyorker_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-newyorker-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mimisales2_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-mimisales2-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-national_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-national-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> Additionally, "All I Want for Christmas Is You" was critically lauded, and is considered "one of the few worthy modern additions to the holiday canon."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newyorker_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-newyorker-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> described it as a "holiday standard", and ranked it fourth on its Greatest Rock and Roll Christmas Songs list.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RSS_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-RSS-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Commercially, it became the best-selling holiday ringtone of all time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newswire_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-newswire-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> and the best-selling single by a non-Asian artist in Japan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> selling over 2.1 million units (both ringtone and digital download).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riajCDcertification1994_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-riajCDcertification1994-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riajdl200812_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-riajdl200812-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> By the end of the holiday season of 1994, Carey and Afanasieff had already begun writing material for her next studio album, which would be released late the following year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson35_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-nickson35-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mariah_Carey_One_Sweet_Day_Madison_Square_1995.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="838" data-file-width="1488" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Mariah_Carey_One_Sweet_Day_Madison_Square_1995.jpg/220px-Mariah_Carey_One_Sweet_Day_Madison_Square_1995.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Mariah_Carey_One_Sweet_Day_Madison_Square_1995.jpg/330px-Mariah_Carey_One_Sweet_Day_Madison_Square_1995.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Mariah_Carey_One_Sweet_Day_Madison_Square_1995.jpg/440px-Mariah_Carey_One_Sweet_Day_Madison_Square_1995.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mariah_Carey_One_Sweet_Day_Madison_Square_1995.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Carey performing "One Sweet Day" alongside <a href="/wiki/Boyz_II_Men" title="Boyz II Men">Boyz II Men</a>, during a taping at <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square_Garden" title="Madison Square Garden">Madison Square Garden</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Released on October 3, 1995, <i><a href="/wiki/Daydream_(Mariah_Carey_album)" title="Daydream (Mariah Carey album)">Daydream</a></i> combined the pop sensibilities of <i>Music Box</i> with downbeat R&amp;B and <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music" title="Hip hop music">hip hop</a> influences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro12_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro12-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> The album's second single, "<a href="/wiki/One_Sweet_Day" title="One Sweet Day">One Sweet Day</a>" was inspired by the death of <a href="/wiki/David_Cole_(record_producer)" title="David Cole (record producer)">David Cole</a>, as well as her sister Alison, who had contracted <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">AIDS</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro14_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro14-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> The song remained atop the Hot 100 for a record-holding 16 weeks, and became the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Billboard_Hot_100_chart_achievements_and_milestones" title="List of Billboard Hot 100 chart achievements and milestones">longest-running number-one song in history</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson36_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-nickson36-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Daydream</i> became her biggest-selling album in the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> and became her second album to be certified <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RIAA_Certification" title="RIAA Certification">Diamond</a> by the RIAA, following <i>Music Box</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riaa_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-riaa-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> The album again was the best-seller by an international artist in Japan, shipping over 2.2 million copies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> and eventually reaching global sales of over 25 million units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bet_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-bet-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> Critically, the album was heralded as Carey's best to date; <i>The New York Times</i> named it one of 1995's best albums, and wrote, "best cuts bring R&amp;B candy-making to a new peak of textural refinement [...] Carey's songwriting has taken a leap forward and become more relaxed, sexier and less reliant on thudding clichés."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> Carey once again opted to embark on a short world tour titled <a href="/wiki/Daydream_World_Tour" title="Daydream World Tour">Daydream World Tour</a>. It had seven dates, three in Japan and four throughout Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson100_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-nickson100-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> When tickets went on sale, Carey set records when all 150,000 tickets for her three shows at Japan's largest stadium, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Dome" title="Tokyo Dome">Tokyo Dome</a>, sold out in under three hours, breaking the previous record held by <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones" title="The Rolling Stones">The Rolling Stones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson100_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-nickson100-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Due to the album's success, Carey won two awards at the <a href="/wiki/American_Music_Award" title="American Music Award">American Music Awards</a> for her solo efforts: <a href="/wiki/American_Music_Award_for_Favorite_Pop/Rock_Female_Artist" title="American Music Award for Favorite Pop/Rock Female Artist">Favorite Pop/Rock Female Artist</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_Music_Award_for_Favorite_Soul/R%26B_Female_Artist" title="American Music Award for Favorite Soul/R&amp;B Female Artist">Favorite Soul/R&amp;B Female Artist</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Daydream</i> and its singles were respectively nominated in six categories at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/38th_Grammy_Awards" title="38th Grammy Awards">38th Grammy Awards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro16_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro16-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> Carey, along with <a href="/wiki/Boyz_II_Men" title="Boyz II Men">Boyz II Men</a>, opened the event with a performance of "One Sweet Day".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro17_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro17-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> However, Carey did not receive any award, prompting her to comment "What can you do? I will never be disappointed again. After I sat through the whole show and didn't win once, I can handle anything."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro17_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro17-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1995, due to <i>Daydream</i> '​s enormous Japanese sales, <i>Billboard</i> declared Carey the "Overseas Artist of the Year" in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1997.E2.80.932000:_New_image_and_independence.2C_Butterfly.2C_and_Rainbow">1997–2000: New image and independence, <i>Butterfly</i>, and <i>Rainbow</i></span></h3>
<p>With her following albums, Carey began to take more initiative and control with her music, and started infusing more genres into her work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson39_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-nickson39-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> During mid-1997, Carey was well underway, writing and recording material for her next album, <i><a href="/wiki/Butterfly_(Mariah_Carey_album)" title="Butterfly (Mariah Carey album)">Butterfly</a></i> (1997).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson40_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-nickson40-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> She sought to work with other producers and writers other than Afanasieff, such as <a href="/wiki/Sean_Combs" title="Sean Combs">Sean Combs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kamaal_Fareed" title="Kamaal Fareed">Kamaal Fareed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Missy_Elliott" title="Missy Elliott">Missy Elliott</a> and Jean Claude Oliver and Samuel Barnes from <a href="/wiki/Trackmasters" title="Trackmasters">Trackmasters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson40_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-nickson40-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> During the album's recording, Carey and Mottola separated, with Carey citing it as her way of achieving freedom, and a new lease on life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson41_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-nickson41-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> Aside from the album's different approach, critics took notice of Carey's altered style of singing, which she described as <a href="/wiki/Breathy_voice" title="Breathy voice">breathy vocals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> Her new-found style of singing was met with mixed reception; some critics felt is was a sign of maturity, that she did not feel the need to always show off her upper range,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buff_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-buff-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> while others felt it was a sign of her weakening and waning voice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro18_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro18-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> The album's lead single, "<a href="/wiki/Honey_(Mariah_Carey_song)" title="Honey (Mariah Carey song)">Honey</a>", and its accompanying music video, introduced a more overtly sexual image than Carey had ever demonstrated, and furthered reports of her freedom from Mottola.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro19_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro19-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> Carey stated that <i>Butterfly</i> marked the point when she attained full creative control over her music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fox_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-fox-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> However, she added, "I don't think that it's that much of a departure from what I've done in the past [...] It's not like I went psycho and thought I would be a rapper. Personally, this album is about doing whatever the hell I wanted to do."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro19_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro19-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> Growing creative differences with producer Afanasieff continued, and eventually ended their working relationship, after collaborating on most of Carey's material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro20_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro20-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> Reviews for <i>Butterfly</i> were generally positive: <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> wrote, "It's not as if Carey has totally dispensed with her old saccharine, Houston-style balladry [...] but the predominant mood of 'Butterfly' is one of coolly erotic reverie. [... Except "Outside" the album sounds] very 1997. [...] Carey has spread her wings and she's ready to fly."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/AllMusic" title="AllMusic">AllMusic</a> editor <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Thomas_Erlewine" title="Stephen Thomas Erlewine">Stephen Thomas Erlewine</a> described Carey's vocals as "sultrier and more controlled than ever", and heralded <i>Butterfly</i> as one of her "best records and illustrates that Carey continues to improve and refine her music, which makes her a rarity among her '90s peers.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-all_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-all-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> The album was a commercial success, although not to the degree of her previous three albums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro20_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro20-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mariah_Carey12_Edwards_Dec_1998.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="692" data-file-width="470" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Mariah_Carey12_Edwards_Dec_1998.jpg/170px-Mariah_Carey12_Edwards_Dec_1998.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Mariah_Carey12_Edwards_Dec_1998.jpg/255px-Mariah_Carey12_Edwards_Dec_1998.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Mariah_Carey12_Edwards_Dec_1998.jpg/340px-Mariah_Carey12_Edwards_Dec_1998.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mariah_Carey12_Edwards_Dec_1998.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Carey at the Edwards Air Force Base during the making of the "<a href="/wiki/I_Still_Believe_(Brenda_K._Starr_song)" title="I Still Believe (Brenda K. Starr song)">I Still Believe</a>" music video in December 1998.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Toward the turn of the millennium, Carey began developing other projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro21_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro21-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> On April 14, 1998, Carey partook in the <a href="/wiki/VH1_Divas" title="VH1 Divas">VH1 Divas</a> <a href="/wiki/Benefit_concert" title="Benefit concert">benefit concert</a>, where she sang alongside <a href="/wiki/Aretha_Franklin" title="Aretha Franklin">Aretha Franklin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celine_Dion" title="Celine Dion">Celine Dion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shania_Twain" title="Shania Twain">Shania Twain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gloria_Estefan" title="Gloria Estefan">Gloria Estefan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carole_King" title="Carole King">Carole King</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-divas_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-divas-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Carey had begun developing a film project <i>All That Glitters</i>, later re-titled to simply <i><a href="/wiki/Glitter_(film)" title="Glitter (film)">Glitter</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boston_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-boston-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (February 2015)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup> and intended her songwriting to other projects, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Men_in_Black_(film)" title="Men in Black (film)">Men in Black</a></i> (1997) and <i><a href="/wiki/Dr._Seuss%27_How_the_Grinch_Stole_Christmas_(film)" title="Dr. Seuss' How the Grinch Stole Christmas (film)">How the Grinch Stole Christmas</a></i> (2000).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro21_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro21-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> After <i>Glitter</i> fell into <a href="/wiki/Development_hell" title="Development hell">developmental hell</a>, Carey postponed the project, and began writing material for a new album.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro21_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro21-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> The executives at <a href="/wiki/Sony_Music_Entertainment" title="Sony Music Entertainment">Sony Music</a>, the parent company of Carey's label Columbia, wanted her to prepare a greatest hits collection in time for the commercially favorable holiday season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro22_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro22-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> However, they disagreed as to what content and singles should constitute the album.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro23_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro23-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> Sony wanted to release an album that featured her number one singles in the United States, and her international chart toppers on the European versions, void of any new material, while Carey felt that a compilation album should reflect on her most personal songs, not just her most commercial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro23_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro23-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> She felt that not including any new material would result in cheating her fans, therefore including four new songs that she had recorded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro23_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro23-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> While compromised, Carey often expressed distaste towards the album's song selection, expressing her disappointment in the omission of her "favorite songs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vinsky_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-vinsky-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> The album, titled <i><a href="/wiki/Number_1%27s_(Mariah_Carey_album)" title="Number 1's (Mariah Carey album)">#1's</a></i> (1998), featured a duet with <a href="/wiki/Whitney_Houston" title="Whitney Houston">Whitney Houston</a>, "<a href="/wiki/When_You_Believe" title="When You Believe">When You Believe</a>", which was included on the soundtrack for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prince_of_Egypt" title="The Prince of Egypt">The Prince of Egypt</a></i> (1998).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro23_98-3"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro23-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> During the development of <i>All That Glitters</i>, Carey had been introduced to <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks" title="DreamWorks">DreamWorks</a> producer <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Katzenberg" title="Jeffrey Katzenberg">Jeffrey Katzenberg</a>, who asked her if she would record the song "When You Believe" for the soundtrack to the animated film <i>The Prince of Egypt</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mtvbelieve_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-mtvbelieve-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> In an interview with <i><a href="/wiki/Ebony_(magazine)" title="Ebony (magazine)">Ebony</a></i>, Houston described working with Carey, as well as their growing friendship: "Mariah and I got along very great. We had never talked and never sang together before. We just had a chance for camaraderie, singer-to-singer, artist-to-artist, that kind of thing. We just laughed and talked and laughed and talked and sang in between that ... It's good to know that two ladies of soul and music can still be friends."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>#1's</i> became a phenomenon in Japan, selling over one million copies in its opening week, and placing as the only international artist to accomplish this feat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-japansales_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-japansales-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> When describing Carey's popularity in Japan throughout the 1990s, author Chris Nickson compared it to <a href="/wiki/Beatlemania" title="Beatlemania">Beatlemania</a> in the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickson100_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-nickson100-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> The album sold over 3.25 million copies in Japan after only the first three months, and holds the record as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_albums_in_Japan" title="List of best-selling albums in Japan">best-selling album</a> by a non-Asian artist,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-japansales_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-japansales-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> while amassing global sales of over 17 million copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>During the spring of 1999, Carey began working on the final album of her record contract with Sony, her ex-husband's label.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro25_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro25-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> During this time, Carey's strained relationship with Sony affected her work with writing partner Afanasieff, who had worked extensively with Carey throughout the first half of her career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro25_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro25-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> She felt Mottola was trying to separate her from Afanasieff, in hopes of keeping their relationship permanently strained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro26_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro26-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> Due to the pressure and the awkward relationship Carey had now developed with Sony, she completed the album in a period of three months in the summer of 1999, quicker than any of her other albums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro6_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro6-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> The album, titled <i><a href="/wiki/Rainbow_(Mariah_Carey_album)" title="Rainbow (Mariah Carey album)">Rainbow</a></i> (1999), found Carey once again working with a new array of music producers and songwriters, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jay-Z" title="Jay-Z">Jay-Z</a> and <a href="/wiki/DJ_Clue%3F" title="DJ Clue?">DJ Clue?</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro27_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro27-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> Carey also wrote two ballads with <a href="/wiki/David_Foster" title="David Foster">David Foster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diane_Warren" title="Diane Warren">Diane Warren</a>, whom she seemingly used to replace Afanasieff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro27_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro27-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Rainbow</i> was released on November 2, 1999, to the highest first week sales of her career at the time, however debuting at number two on the <i>Billboard</i> 200.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro30_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro30-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> Throughout early-2000, Carey's troubled relationship with Columbia grew, as they halted promotion after the album's first two singles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro30_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro30-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> They felt <i>Rainbow</i> didn't have any strong single to be released, whereas Carey wanted a ballad regarding personal and inner strength released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro30_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro30-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> The difference in opinion led to a very public feud, as Carey began posting messages on her webpage in early and mid-2000, telling fans inside information on the dispute, as well as instructing them to request "<a href="/wiki/Can%27t_Take_That_Away_(Mariah%27s_Theme)" title="Can't Take That Away (Mariah's Theme)">Can't Take That Away (Mariah's Theme)</a>" on radio stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro28_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro28-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> One of the messages Carey left on her page read: "Basically, a lot of you know the political situation in my professional career is not positive. It's been really, really hard. I don't even know if this message is going to get to you because I don't know if they want you to hear this. I'm getting a lot of negative feedback from certain corporate people. But I am not willing to give up."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro29_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro29-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> Fearing to lose their label's highest seller, Sony chose to release the song.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro29_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro29-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> Carey, initially content with the agreement, soon found out that the song had only been given a very limited and low-promotion release, which made charting extremely difficult and unlikely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro29_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro29-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> Critical reception of <i>Rainbow</i> was generally enthusiastic, with the <i><a href="/wiki/Sunday_Herald" title="Sunday Herald">Sunday Herald</a></i> saying that the album "sees her impressively tottering between soul ballads and collaborations with R&amp;B heavyweights like <a href="/wiki/Snoop_Dogg" title="Snoop Dogg">Snoop Doggy Dogg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Usher_(entertainer)" title="Usher (entertainer)">Usher</a> [...] It's a polished collection of pop-soul."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Vibe</i> magazine expressed similar sentiments, writing, "She pulls out all stops [...] <i>Rainbow</i> will garner even more adoration".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vibe_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-vibe-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> Though a commercial success, <i>Rainbow</i> became Carey's lowest selling album to that point in her career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fox_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-fox-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2001.E2.80.9304:_Glitter.2C_Charmbracelet.2C_personal_and_professional_struggles">2001–04: <i>Glitter</i>, <i>Charmbracelet</i>, personal and professional struggles</span></h3>
<p>After she received <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Music_Award" title="Billboard Music Award"><i>Billboard'</i>s Artist of the Decade Award</a> and the World Music Award for Best-Selling Female Artist of the Millennium,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bestselling_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-bestselling-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> Carey parted from Columbia and signed a $100 million five-album recording contract with <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Records" title="Virgin Records">Virgin Records</a> (<a href="/wiki/EMI_Records" title="EMI Records">EMI Records</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emi_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-emi-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> Carey was given full conceptual and creative control over the project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emi_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-emi-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> She opted to record an album partly mixed with 1980s influenced disco and other similar genres, in order to go hand-in-hand with the film's setting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> She often stated that Columbia had regarded her as a commodity, with her separation from Mottola exacerbating her relations with label executives. Just a few months later, in July 2001, it was widely reported that Carey had suffered a physical and emotional breakdown. She had left messages on her website that complained of being overworked,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> and her relationship with the Latin icon <a href="/wiki/Luis_Miguel" title="Luis Miguel">Luis Miguel</a> ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (February 2015)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup> In an interview the following year, she said, "I was with people who didn't really know me and I had no personal assistant. I'd do interviews all day long and get two hours of sleep a night, if that."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stand_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-stand-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> Due to the pressure from the media, her heavy work schedule and the split from Miguel, Carey began posting a series of disturbing messages on her official website, and displayed erratic behavior on several live promotional outings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyc_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyc-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> On July 19, 2001, Carey made a surprise appearance on the <a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">MTV</a> program <i><a href="/wiki/Total_Request_Live" title="Total Request Live">Total Request Live</a></i> (TRL).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hospital_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-hospital-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> As the show's host <a href="/wiki/Carson_Daly" title="Carson Daly">Carson Daly</a> began taping following a commercial break, Carey came out pushing an ice cream cart while wearing a large men's shirt, and began a <a href="/wiki/Striptease" title="Striptease">striptease</a>, in which she shed her shirt to reveal a tight yellow and green ensemble.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hospital_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-hospital-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> While she later revealed that Daly was aware of her presence in the building prior to her appearance, Carey's appearance on TRL garnered strong media attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyc_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyc-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> Only days later, Carey began posting irregular voice notes and messages on her official website: "I'm trying to understand things in life right now and so I really don't feel that I should be doing music right now. What I'd like to do is just a take a little break or at least get one night of sleep without someone popping up about a video. All I really want is [to] just be me and that's what I should have done in the first place ... I don't say this much but guess what, I don't take care of myself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hospital_120-2"><a href="#cite_note-hospital-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> Following the quick removal of the messages, Berger commented that Carey had been "obviously exhausted and not thinking clearly" when she posted the letters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hospital2_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-hospital2-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mariah_Carey_2003_tour_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="314" data-file-width="524" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Mariah_Carey_2003_tour_1.jpg/220px-Mariah_Carey_2003_tour_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Mariah_Carey_2003_tour_1.jpg/330px-Mariah_Carey_2003_tour_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Mariah_Carey_2003_tour_1.jpg/440px-Mariah_Carey_2003_tour_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mariah_Carey_2003_tour_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Carey performing "Hero" during her Charmbracelet World Tour</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On July 26, she was suddenly hospitalized, citing "extreme exhaustion" and a "physical and emotional breakdown".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-respect_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-respect-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> Carey was inducted at an un-disclosed hospital in Connecticut, and remained hospitalized and under doctor's care for two weeks, followed by an extended absence from the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-respect_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-respect-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> Following the heavy media coverage surrounding Carey's publicized breakdown and hospitalization, <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Records" title="Virgin Records">Virgin Records</a> and <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">20th Century Fox</a> delayed the release of both <i><a href="/wiki/Glitter_(film)" title="Glitter (film)">Glitter</a></i>, as well as its soundtrack of the same name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hospital2_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-hospital2-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> Consequently, critics suggested that in delaying <i>Glitter</i>, hype for the project would have largely subsided, and would possibly hurt both ticket and album sales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-daily2_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-daily2-123"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (February 2015)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup> When discussing the project's weak commercial reaction, Carey blamed both her frame of mind during the time of its release, its postponement, as well as the soundtrack having been released on <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> Critics panned <i><a href="/wiki/Glitter_(film)" title="Glitter (film)">Glitter</a></i>, as well as its accompanying soundtrack; both were unsuccessful commercially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (February 2015)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup> The accompanying soundtrack album, <i><a href="/wiki/Glitter_(soundtrack)" title="Glitter (soundtrack)">Glitter</a></i>, became Carey's lowest-selling album to that point. The <i><a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Post-Dispatch" title="St. Louis Post-Dispatch">St. Louis Post-Dispatch</a></i> dismissed it as "an absolute mess that'll go down as an annoying blemish on a career that, while not always critically heralded, was at least nearly consistently successful."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> Following the negative cloud that was ensuing Carey's personal life at the time, as well as the project's poor reception, her $100 million five-album record deal with <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Records" title="Virgin Records">Virgin Records</a> (<a href="/wiki/EMI_Records" title="EMI Records">EMI Records</a>) was bought out for $50 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emi_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-emi-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-contract_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-contract-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> Soon after, Carey flew to <a href="/wiki/Capri" title="Capri">Capri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> for a period of five months, in which she began writing material for her new album, stemming from all the personal experiences she had endured throughout the past year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyc_119-2"><a href="#cite_note-nyc-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> Carey later said that her time at Virgin was "a complete and total stress-fest [...] I made a total snap decision which was based on money and I never make decisions based on money. I learned a big lesson from that."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> Later that year, she signed a contract with <a href="/wiki/Island_Records" title="Island Records">Island Records</a>, valued at more than $24 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> and launched the record label <a href="/wiki/Monarc_Entertainment" title="Monarc Entertainment">MonarC</a>. To add further to Carey's emotional burdens, her father, with whom she had little contact since childhood, died of <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a> that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 2002, Carey was cast in the independent film, <i><a href="/wiki/WiseGirls" title="WiseGirls">WiseGirls</a></i>, alongside <a href="/wiki/Mira_Sorvino" title="Mira Sorvino">Mira Sorvino</a> and <a href="/wiki/Melora_Walters" title="Melora Walters">Melora Walters</a>, who co-starred as waitresses at a mobster-operated restaurant. It premiered at the <a href="/wiki/Sundance_Film_Festival" title="Sundance Film Festival">Sundance Film Festival</a>, and received generally negative critical response, though Carey's portrayal of the character was praised; Roger Friedman of <a href="/wiki/Fox_News_Channel" title="Fox News Channel">Fox News</a> referred to her as "a <a href="/wiki/Thelma_Ritter" title="Thelma Ritter">Thelma Ritter</a> for the new millennium", and wrote, "Her line delivery is sharp and she manages to get the right laughs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup> Later that year, Carey performed the American <a href="/wiki/The_Star-Spangled_Banner" title="The Star-Spangled Banner">national anthem</a> to rave reviews at the <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_XXXVI" title="Super Bowl XXXVI">Super Bowl XXXVI</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louisiana_Superdome" title="Louisiana Superdome">Louisiana Superdome</a> in New Orleans, Louisiana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-europe_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-europe-132"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> Towards the end of 2002, Carey released her next studio album <i><a href="/wiki/Charmbracelet" title="Charmbracelet">Charmbracelet</a></i>, which she said marked "a new lease on life" for her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stand_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-stand-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> Though released in the wake of <i>Glitter</i> and Carey's return to the music scene, sales of <i>Charmbracelet</i> were moderate and the quality of Carey's vocals came under criticism. Joan Anderson from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Boston_Globe" title="The Boston Globe">The Boston Globe</a></i> declared the album "the worst of her career, and revealed a voice [that is] no longer capable of either gravity-defying gymnastics or soft coos",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/AllMusic" title="AllMusic">AllMusic</a> editor <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Thomas_Erlewine" title="Stephen Thomas Erlewine">Stephen Thomas Erlewine</a> expressed similar sentiments and wrote, "What is a greater problem is that Mariah's voice is shot, sounding in tatters throughout the record. She can no longer coo or softly croon nor can she perform her trademark gravity-defying vocal runs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup> In an attempt to "relaunch" her career following the poor reception to <i>Glitter</i>, as well as her breakdown, Carey announced a world tour in April 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vh1_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-vh1-135"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (February 2015)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup> Lasting over eight months, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charmbracelet_World_Tour:_An_Intimate_Evening_with_Mariah_Carey" title="Charmbracelet World Tour: An Intimate Evening with Mariah Carey">Charmbracelet World Tour: An Intimate Evening with Mariah Carey</a>, became her most extensive tour to date, spanning sixty-nine shows around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vh2_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-vh2-136"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (February 2015)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup> Throughout the United States, the shows were done in smaller theaters, and something more Broadway-influenced, "It's much more intimate so you'll feel like you had an experience. You experience a night with me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vh1_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-vh1-135"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> However, while smaller productions were booked throughout the tour's stateside leg, Carey performed at stadiums in Asia and Europe, performing for a crowd of over 35,000 in Manila, 50,000 in Malaysia, and to over 70,000 people in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> In the United Kingdom, it became Carey's first tour to feature shows outside of London, booking arena stops in Glasgow, Birmingham and Manchester.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> Charmbracelet World Tour: An Intimate Evening with Mariah Carey garnered generally positive reviews from music critics and concert goers, with many complimenting the quality of Carey's live vocals, as well as the production as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asilo_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-asilo-139"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2005.E2.80.9307:_Commercial_success_with_The_Emancipation_of_Mimi">2005–07: Commercial success with <i>The Emancipation of Mimi</i></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mcla_re03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2825" data-file-width="2125" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Mcla_re03.jpg/170px-Mcla_re03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Mcla_re03.jpg/255px-Mcla_re03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Mcla_re03.jpg/340px-Mcla_re03.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mcla_re03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Carey, pictured with former <a href="/wiki/Island_Records" title="Island Records">Island Records</a> head <a href="/wiki/L.A._Reid" title="L.A. Reid">L.A. Reid</a> in 2005, at the release party for <i>The Emancipation of Mimi</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Throughout 2004, Carey focused on composing material for her tenth studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Emancipation_of_Mimi" title="The Emancipation of Mimi">The Emancipation of Mimi</a></i> (2005).
